<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Gunpowder</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Explosive once used in firearms</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Gunpowder_(disambiguation)" title="Gunpowder (disambiguation)">Gunpowder (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Veterans_Weekend,_Hull_-_panoramio.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="608" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Veterans_Weekend%2C_Hull_-_panoramio.jpg/300px-Veterans_Weekend%2C_Hull_-_panoramio.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Veterans_Weekend%2C_Hull_-_panoramio.jpg/450px-Veterans_Weekend%2C_Hull_-_panoramio.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Veterans_Weekend%2C_Hull_-_panoramio.jpg/600px-Veterans_Weekend%2C_Hull_-_panoramio.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Veterans_Weekend,_Hull_-_panoramio.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Re-enactors <a href="/wiki/Volley_fire" title="Volley fire">volley firing</a> with black powder</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Black_Powder-1.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Black_Powder-1.JPG/300px-Black_Powder-1.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Black_Powder-1.JPG/450px-Black_Powder-1.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Black_Powder-1.JPG/600px-Black_Powder-1.JPG 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Black_Powder-1.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gunpowder for <a href="/wiki/Muzzleloader" title="Muzzleloader">muzzleloading</a> firearms in granulation size</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Z%C3%BCndkraut_mit_Z%C3%BCndkrautspender.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1309" data-file-width="2283" decoding="async" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Z%C3%BCndkraut_mit_Z%C3%BCndkrautspender.jpg/300px-Z%C3%BCndkraut_mit_Z%C3%BCndkrautspender.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Z%C3%BCndkraut_mit_Z%C3%BCndkrautspender.jpg/450px-Z%C3%BCndkraut_mit_Z%C3%BCndkrautspender.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Z%C3%BCndkraut_mit_Z%C3%BCndkrautspender.jpg/600px-Z%C3%BCndkraut_mit_Z%C3%BCndkrautspender.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Z%C3%BCndkraut_mit_Z%C3%BCndkrautspender.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Flash_pan" title="Flash pan">Flash pan</a> starter dispenser</div></div></div>
<p><b>Gunpowder</b>, also commonly known as <b>black powder</b> to distinguish it from modern <a href="/wiki/Smokeless_powder" title="Smokeless powder">smokeless powder</a>, is the earliest known chemical <a href="/wiki/Explosive" title="Explosive">explosive</a>. It consists of a mixture of <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> (in the form of <a href="/wiki/Charcoal" title="Charcoal">charcoal</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Potassium_nitrate" title="Potassium nitrate">potassium nitrate</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saltpeter" title="Saltpeter">saltpeter</a>). The sulfur and carbon act as <a href="/wiki/Fuel" title="Fuel">fuels</a> while the saltpeter is an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxidizer" title="Oxidizer">oxidizer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAgrawal201069_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAgrawal201069-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECressy2013_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECressy2013-2">[2]</a></sup> Gunpowder has been widely used as a <a href="/wiki/Propellant" title="Propellant">propellant</a> in <a href="/wiki/Firearm" title="Firearm">firearms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Artillery" title="Artillery">artillery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rocket" title="Rocket">rocketry</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pyrotechnics" title="Pyrotechnics">pyrotechnics</a>, including use as a blasting agent for <a href="/wiki/Explosive" title="Explosive">explosives</a> in <a href="/wiki/Quarry" title="Quarry">quarrying</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mining" title="Mining">mining</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Road#Construction" title="Road">road building</a>.
</p><p>Gunpowder is classified as a <a href="/wiki/Explosive#Low" title="Explosive">low explosive</a> because of its relatively slow decomposition rate and consequently low <a href="/wiki/Brisance" title="Brisance">brisance</a>. Low explosives <a href="/wiki/Deflagration" title="Deflagration">deflagrate</a> (i.e., burn at subsonic speeds), whereas <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_explosives" title="High explosives">high explosives</a> <a href="/wiki/Detonation" title="Detonation">detonate</a>, producing a supersonic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shockwave" title="Shockwave">shockwave</a>. Ignition of gunpowder packed behind a <a href="/wiki/Projectile" title="Projectile">projectile</a> generates enough pressure to force the shot from the muzzle at high speed, but usually not enough force to rupture the <a href="/wiki/Gun_barrel" title="Gun barrel">gun barrel</a>. It thus makes a good propellant, but is less suitable for shattering rock or fortifications with its low-yield explosive power. Nonetheless it was widely used to fill fused artillery shells (and used in <a href="/wiki/Mining" title="Mining">mining</a> and <a href="/wiki/Civil_engineering" title="Civil engineering">civil engineering</a> projects) until the second half of the 19th century, when the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_explosive" title="High explosive">high explosives</a> were put into use.
</p><p>Its use in weapons has declined due to smokeless powder replacing it, and it is no longer used for industrial purposes due to its relative inefficiency compared to newer alternatives such as <a href="/wiki/Dynamite" title="Dynamite">dynamite</a> and <a href="/wiki/ANFO" title="ANFO">ammonium nitrate/fuel oil</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rossotti2002_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rossotti2002-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Effect">Effect</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gunpowder&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Effect">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Gunpowder is a <a href="/wiki/Explosive#Low" title="Explosive">low explosive</a>: it does not <a href="/wiki/Detonation" title="Detonation">detonate</a>, but rather <a href="/wiki/Deflagration" title="Deflagration">deflagrates</a> (burns quickly). This is an advantage in a propellant device, where one does not desire a shock that would shatter the gun and potentially harm the operator; however, it is a drawback when an explosion is desired. In that case, the propellant (and most importantly, gases produced by its burning) must be confined. Since it contains its own oxidizer and additionally burns faster under pressure, its combustion is capable of bursting containers such as a shell, grenade, or improvised "pipe bomb" or "pressure cooker" casings to form <a href="/wiki/Fragmentation_(weaponry)" title="Fragmentation (weaponry)">shrapnel</a>.
</p><p>In quarrying, high explosives are generally preferred for shattering rock. However, because of its low <a href="/wiki/Brisance" title="Brisance">brisance</a>, gunpowder causes fewer fractures and results in more usable stone compared to other explosives, making it useful for blasting <a href="/wiki/Slate" title="Slate">slate</a>, which is fragile,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pid_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pid-4">[4]</a></sup> or monumental stone such as <a href="/wiki/Granite" title="Granite">granite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marble" title="Marble">marble</a>. Gunpowder is well suited for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blank_round" title="Blank round">blank rounds</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Signal_flare" title="Signal flare">signal flares</a>, <a href="/wiki/Burst_charge" title="Burst charge">burst charges</a>, and rescue-line launches. It is also used in fireworks for lifting shells, in rockets as fuel, and in certain <a href="/wiki/Special_effect" title="Special effect">special effects</a>.
</p><p>Combustion converts less than half the mass of gunpowder to gas; most of it turns into particulate matter. Some of it is ejected, wasting propelling power, fouling the air, and generally being a nuisance (giving away a soldier's position, generating fog that hinders vision, etc.). Some of it ends up as a thick layer of <a href="/wiki/Soot" title="Soot">soot</a> inside the barrel, where it also is a nuisance for subsequent shots, and a cause of jamming an automatic weapon. Moreover, this residue is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hygroscopic" title="Hygroscopic">hygroscopic</a>, and with the addition of moisture absorbed from the air forms a <a href="/wiki/Corrosive_substance" title="Corrosive substance">corrosive substance</a>. The soot contains <a href="/wiki/Potassium_oxide" title="Potassium oxide">potassium oxide</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sodium_oxide" title="Sodium oxide">sodium oxide</a> that turns into <a href="/wiki/Potassium_hydroxide" title="Potassium hydroxide">potassium hydroxide</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Sodium_hydroxide" title="Sodium hydroxide">sodium hydroxide</a>, which corrodes <a href="/wiki/Wrought_iron" title="Wrought iron">wrought iron</a> or <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">steel</a> gun barrels. Gunpowder arms therefore require thorough and regular cleaning to remove the residue.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gunpowder&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chinese_Gunpowder_Formula.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="864" data-file-width="1194" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Chinese_Gunpowder_Formula.JPG/220px-Chinese_Gunpowder_Formula.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Chinese_Gunpowder_Formula.JPG/330px-Chinese_Gunpowder_Formula.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Chinese_Gunpowder_Formula.JPG/440px-Chinese_Gunpowder_Formula.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chinese_Gunpowder_Formula.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Earliest known written formula for gunpowder, from the <i><a href="/wiki/Wujing_Zongyao" title="Wujing Zongyao">Wujing Zongyao</a></i> of 1044 AD.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%E3%81%A6%E3%81%A4%E3%81%AF%E3%81%86%EF%BC%88%E9%9C%87%E5%A4%A9%E9%9B%B7%EF%BC%89.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2783" data-file-width="4423" decoding="async" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/%E3%81%A6%E3%81%A4%E3%81%AF%E3%81%86%EF%BC%88%E9%9C%87%E5%A4%A9%E9%9B%B7%EF%BC%89.JPG/220px-%E3%81%A6%E3%81%A4%E3%81%AF%E3%81%86%EF%BC%88%E9%9C%87%E5%A4%A9%E9%9B%B7%EF%BC%89.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/%E3%81%A6%E3%81%A4%E3%81%AF%E3%81%86%EF%BC%88%E9%9C%87%E5%A4%A9%E9%9B%B7%EF%BC%89.JPG/330px-%E3%81%A6%E3%81%A4%E3%81%AF%E3%81%86%EF%BC%88%E9%9C%87%E5%A4%A9%E9%9B%B7%EF%BC%89.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/%E3%81%A6%E3%81%A4%E3%81%AF%E3%81%86%EF%BC%88%E9%9C%87%E5%A4%A9%E9%9B%B7%EF%BC%89.JPG/440px-%E3%81%A6%E3%81%A4%E3%81%AF%E3%81%86%EF%BC%88%E9%9C%87%E5%A4%A9%E9%9B%B7%EF%BC%89.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%E3%81%A6%E3%81%A4%E3%81%AF%E3%81%86%EF%BC%88%E9%9C%87%E5%A4%A9%E9%9B%B7%EF%BC%89.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Stoneware bombs, known in Japanese as <i>Tetsuhau</i> (iron bomb), or in Chinese as <i>Zhentianlei</i> (<a href="/wiki/Thunder_crash_bomb" title="Thunder crash bomb">thunder crash bomb</a>), excavated from the Takashima shipwreck, October 2011, dated to the <a href="/wiki/Mongol_invasions_of_Japan" title="Mongol invasions of Japan">Mongol invasions of Japan</a> (1274–1281 AD).</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_gunpowder" title="History of gunpowder">History of gunpowder</a> and <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_the_gunpowder_age" title="Timeline of the gunpowder age">Timeline of the gunpowder age</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_firearm" title="History of the firearm">History of the firearm</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="China">China</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gunpowder&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: China">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ming_Dynasty_eruptor_proto-cannon.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="818" data-file-width="638" decoding="async" height="282" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Ming_Dynasty_eruptor_proto-cannon.jpg/220px-Ming_Dynasty_eruptor_proto-cannon.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Ming_Dynasty_eruptor_proto-cannon.jpg/330px-Ming_Dynasty_eruptor_proto-cannon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Ming_Dynasty_eruptor_proto-cannon.jpg/440px-Ming_Dynasty_eruptor_proto-cannon.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ming_Dynasty_eruptor_proto-cannon.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 'flying-cloud thunderclap-eruptor' firing thunderclap bombs from the <i><a href="/wiki/Huolongjing" title="Huolongjing">Huolongjing</a></i></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Wujing_Zongyao" title="Wujing Zongyao">Wujing Zongyao</a>, <a href="/wiki/Four_Great_Inventions" title="Four Great Inventions">Four Great Inventions</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_Chinese_inventions" title="List of Chinese inventions">List of Chinese inventions</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Heilongjiang_hand_cannon" title="Heilongjiang hand cannon">Heilongjiang hand cannon</a></div>
<p>The first confirmed reference to what can be considered gunpowder in China occurred in the 9th century AD during the <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang dynasty</a>, first in a formula contained in the <i>Taishang Shengzu Jindan Mijue</i> (太上聖祖金丹秘訣) in 808, and then about 50 years later in a Taoist text known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Zhenyuan_Miaodao_Yaolue" title="Zhenyuan Miaodao Yaolue">Zhenyuan miaodao yaolüe</a></i> (真元妙道要略).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELorge200832_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELorge200832-5">[5]</a></sup> The <i>Taishang Shengzu Jindan Mijue</i> mentions a formula composed of six parts sulfur to six parts saltpeter to one part birthwort herb.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELorge200832_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELorge200832-5">[5]</a></sup> According to the <i>Zhenyuan miaodao yaolüe</i>, "Some have heated together sulfur, <a href="/wiki/Realgar" title="Realgar">realgar</a> and saltpeter with <a href="/wiki/Honey" title="Honey">honey</a>; smoke and flames result, so that their hands and faces have been burnt, and even the whole house where they were working burned down."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKelly20044_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKelly20044-6">[6]</a></sup> Based on these Taoist texts, the invention of gunpowder by Chinese alchemists was likely an accidental byproduct from experiments seeking to create the <a href="/wiki/Elixir_of_life" title="Elixir of life">elixir of life</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChase200331–32Andrade201630_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChase200331–32Andrade201630-7">[7]</a></sup> This <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Experimental_medicine" title="Experimental medicine">experimental medicine</a> origin is reflected in its Chinese name <i>huoyao</i> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">火药/火藥</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">huǒ yào</span></i> <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/xuo yɑʊ/</span>), which means "fire medicine".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAndrade201630_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAndrade201630-8">[8]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saltpeter" title="Saltpeter">Saltpeter</a> was known to the Chinese by the mid-1st century AD and was primarily produced in the provinces of <a href="/wiki/Sichuan" title="Sichuan">Sichuan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shanxi" title="Shanxi">Shanxi</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Shandong" title="Shandong">Shandong</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986103_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986103-9">[9]</a></sup> There is strong evidence of the use of saltpeter and sulfur in various <a href="/wiki/Medicine" title="Medicine">medicinal</a> combinations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBuchanan2006_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBuchanan2006-10">[10]</a></sup> A Chinese alchemical text dated 492 noted saltpeter burnt with a purple flame, providing a practical and reliable means of distinguishing it from other inorganic salts, thus enabling alchemists to evaluate and compare purification techniques; the earliest Latin accounts of saltpeter purification are dated after 1200.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChase200331–32_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChase200331–32-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>The earliest chemical formula for gunpowder appeared in the 11th century <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">Song dynasty</a> text, <i><a href="/wiki/Wujing_Zongyao" title="Wujing Zongyao">Wujing Zongyao</a></i> (<i>Complete Essentials from the Military Classics</i>), written by <a href="/wiki/Zeng_Gongliang" title="Zeng Gongliang">Zeng Gongliang</a> between 1040 and 1044.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChase200331_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChase200331-12">[12]</a></sup> The <i>Wujing Zongyao</i> provides encyclopedia references to a variety of mixtures that included petrochemicals—as well as garlic and honey. A slow match for flame throwing mechanisms using the siphon principle and for fireworks and rockets is mentioned. The mixture formulas in this book do not contain enough saltpeter to create an explosive however; being limited to at most 50% saltpeter, they produce an <a href="/wiki/Incendiary_device" title="Incendiary device">incendiary</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChase200331_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChase200331-12">[12]</a></sup> The <i>Essentials</i> was written by a <a href="/wiki/Science_and_technology_of_the_Song_dynasty" title="Science and technology of the Song dynasty">Song dynasty</a> court bureaucrat and there is little evidence that it had any immediate impact on warfare; there is no mention of its use in the chronicles of the wars against the <a href="/wiki/Tangut_people" title="Tangut people">Tanguts</a> in the 11th century, and China was otherwise mostly at peace during this century. However it had already been used for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fire_arrows" title="Fire arrows">fire arrows</a> since at least the 10th century. Its first recorded military application dates its use to the year 904 in the form of incendiary projectiles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAndrade201631_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAndrade201631-13">[13]</a></sup> In the following centuries various gunpowder weapons such as <a href="/wiki/Bomb" title="Bomb">bombs</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fire_lances" title="Fire lances">fire lances</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Gun" title="Gun">gun</a> appeared in China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBuchanan20062_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBuchanan20062-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChase20031_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChase20031-15">[15]</a></sup> Explosive weapons such as bombs have been discovered in a shipwreck off the shore of Japan dated from 1281, during the Mongol invasions of Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>By 1083 the Song court was producing hundreds of thousands of fire arrows for their garrisons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAndrade201632_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAndrade201632-17">[17]</a></sup> Bombs and the first proto-guns, known as "fire lances", became prominent during the 12th century and were used by the Song during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jin-Song_Wars" title="Jin-Song Wars">Jin-Song Wars</a>. Fire lances were first recorded to have been used at the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_De%27an" title="Siege of De'an">Siege of De'an</a> in 1132 by Song forces against the <a href="/wiki/Jin_dynasty_(1115%E2%80%931234)" title="Jin dynasty (1115–1234)">Jin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELorge200833–34_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELorge200833–34-18">[18]</a></sup> In the early 13th century the Jin utilized iron-casing bombs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAndrade201642_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAndrade201642-19">[19]</a></sup> Projectiles were added to fire lances, and re-usable fire lance barrels were developed, first out of hardened paper, and then metal. By 1257 some fire lances were firing wads of bullets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAndrade201651_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAndrade201651-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPartington1960246_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPartington1960246-21">[21]</a></sup> In the late 13th century metal fire lances became 'eruptors', proto-cannons firing co-viative projectiles (mixed with the propellant, rather than seated over it with a wad), and by 1287 at the latest, had become true guns, the <a href="/wiki/Hand_cannon" title="Hand cannon">hand cannon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986293–94_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986293–94-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_East">Middle East</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gunpowder&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Middle East">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/List_of_inventions_in_the_medieval_Islamic_world" title="List of inventions in the medieval Islamic world">List of inventions in the medieval Islamic world</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alchemy_and_chemistry_in_medieval_Islam" title="Alchemy and chemistry in medieval Islam">Alchemy and chemistry in medieval Islam</a></div>
<p>According to Iqtidar Alam Khan, it was invading Mongols who introduced gunpowder to the Islamic world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKhan1996_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKhan1996-23">[23]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslims</a> acquired knowledge of gunpowder some time between 1240 and 1280, by which point the Syrian <a href="/wiki/Hasan_al-Rammah" title="Hasan al-Rammah">Hasan al-Rammah</a> had written recipes, instructions for the purification of saltpeter, and descriptions of gunpowder incendiaries. It is implied by al-Rammah's usage of "terms that suggested he derived his knowledge from Chinese sources" and his references to saltpeter as "Chinese snow" (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="ar">ثلج الصين</span> <i><span title="DIN 31635 Arabic (Arabic language) transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">thalj al-ṣīn</i></span></i>), fireworks as "Chinese flowers", and rockets as "Chinese arrows" that knowledge of gunpowder arrived from China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKelly200422_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKelly200422-24">[24]</a></sup> However, because al-Rammah attributes his material to "his father and forefathers", <a href="/wiki/Ahmad_Y._al-Hassan" title="Ahmad Y. al-Hassan">al-Hassan</a> argues that gunpowder became prevalent in Syria and Egypt by "the end of the twelfth century or the beginning of the thirteenth".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AhmedY_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-AhmedY-25">[25]</a></sup> In Persia saltpeter was known as "Chinese salt" (<a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="fa">نمک چینی</span>) <i>namak-i chīnī</i>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWatson2006304_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWatson2006304-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENolan2006365_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENolan2006365-27">[27]</a></sup> or "salt from Chinese salt marshes" (<span title="Persian-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="fa">نمک شوره چینی</span></span> <i><span title="DIN 31635 Arabic (Persian language) transliteration"><i lang="fa-Latn">namak-i shūra-yi chīnī</i></span></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPartington1960335_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPartington1960335-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1980194_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1980194-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>Hasan al-Rammah included 107 gunpowder recipes in his text <i>al-Furusiyyah wa al-Manasib al-Harbiyya</i> (<i>The Book of Military Horsemanship and Ingenious War Devices</i>), 22 of which are for rockets. If one takes the median of 17 of these 22 compositions for rockets (75% nitrates, 9.06% sulfur, and 15.94% charcoal), it is nearly identical to the modern reported ideal recipe of 75% potassium nitrate, 10% sulfur, and 15% charcoal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AhmedY_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-AhmedY-25">[25]</a></sup> The text also mentions fuses, incendiary bombs, naphtha pots, fire lances, and an illustration and description of the earliest <a href="/wiki/Torpedo" title="Torpedo">torpedo</a>. The torpedo was called the "egg which moves itself and burns".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zaky_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-zaky-30">[30]</a></sup> Two iron sheets were fastened together and tightened using felt. The flattened pear shaped vessel was filled with gunpowder, metal filings, "good mixtures", two rods, and a large rocket for propulsion. Judging by the illustration, it was evidently supposed to glide across the water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zaky_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-zaky-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986259_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986259-32">[32]</a></sup> Fire lances were used in battles between the Muslims and Mongols in 1299 and 1303.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham198645_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham198645-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>Al-Hassan claims that in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Ain_Jalut" title="Battle of Ain Jalut">Battle of Ain Jalut</a> of 1260, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mamluk_Sultanate_(Cairo)" title="Mamluk Sultanate (Cairo)">Mamluks</a> used against the Mongols, in "the first cannon in history", formula with near-identical ideal composition ratios for explosive gunpowder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AhmedY_25-2"><a href="#cite_note-AhmedY-25">[25]</a></sup> Other historians urge caution regarding claims of Islamic firearms use in the 1204–1324 period as late medieval Arabic texts used the same word for gunpowder, <i>naft</i>, that they used for an earlier incendiary, naphtha.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEÁgoston2008_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEÁgoston2008-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPurton2010_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPurton2010-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>The earliest surviving documentary evidence for cannons in the Islamic world is from an Arabic manuscript dated to the early 14th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> The author's name is uncertain but may have been Shams al-Din Muhammad, who died in 1350.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zaky_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-zaky-30">[30]</a></sup> Dating from around 1320-1350, the illustrations show gunpowder weapons such as gunpowder arrows, bombs, fire tubes, and fire lances or proto-guns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986259_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986259-32">[32]</a></sup> The manuscript describes a type of gunpowder weapon called a <i>midfa</i> which uses gunpowder to shoot projectiles out of a tube at the end of a stock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham198643_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham198643-38">[38]</a></sup> Some consider this to be a cannon while others do not. The problem with identifying cannons in early 14th century Arabic texts is the term <i>midfa</i>, which appears from 1342 to 1352 but cannot be proven to be true hand-guns or bombards. Contemporary accounts of a metal-barrel cannon in the Islamic world do not occur until 1365.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham198644_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham198644-39">[39]</a></sup> Needham believes that in its original form the term <i>midfa</i> refers to the tube or cylinder of a <a href="/wiki/Naphtha" title="Naphtha">naphtha</a> projector (<a href="/wiki/Flamethrower" title="Flamethrower">flamethrower</a>), then after the invention of gunpowder it meant the tube of fire lances, and eventually it applied to the cylinder of hand-gun and cannon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986582_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986582-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Paul E. J. Hammer, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mamluk_Sultanate_(Cairo)" title="Mamluk Sultanate (Cairo)">Mamluks</a> certainly used cannons by 1342.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hammer2017_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hammer2017-41">[41]</a></sup> According to J. Lavin, cannons were used by <a href="/wiki/Moors" title="Moors">Moors</a> at the siege of <a href="/wiki/Algeciras" title="Algeciras">Algeciras</a> in 1343. A metal cannon firing an iron ball was described by Shihab al-Din Abu al-Abbas al-Qalqashandi between 1365-1376.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham198644_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham198644-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Musket" title="Musket">musket</a> appeared in the Ottoman Empire by 1465.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> In 1598, Chinese writer Zhao Shizhen described Turkish muskets as being superior to European muskets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986444_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986444-43">[43]</a></sup> The Chinese military book <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wu_Pei_Chih" title="Wu Pei Chih">Wu Pei Chih</a></i> (1621) later described Turkish muskets that used a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rack-and-pinion" title="Rack-and-pinion">rack-and-pinion</a> mechanism, which was not known to have been used in European or Chinese firearms at the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986446_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986446-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>The state-controlled manufacture of gunpowder by the Ottoman Empire through early <a href="/wiki/Supply_chain" title="Supply chain">supply chains</a> to obtain nitre, sulfur and high-quality charcoal from oaks in <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a> contributed significantly to its expansion between the 15th and 18th century. It was not until later in the 19th century when the syndicalist production of Turkish gunpowder was greatly reduced, which coincided with the decline of its military might.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gunpowder&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:EarlyCannonDeNobilitatibusSapientiiEtPrudentiisRegumManuscriptWalterdeMilemete1326.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1273" data-file-width="2722" decoding="async" height="103" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/EarlyCannonDeNobilitatibusSapientiiEtPrudentiisRegumManuscriptWalterdeMilemete1326.jpg/220px-EarlyCannonDeNobilitatibusSapientiiEtPrudentiisRegumManuscriptWalterdeMilemete1326.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/EarlyCannonDeNobilitatibusSapientiiEtPrudentiisRegumManuscriptWalterdeMilemete1326.jpg/330px-EarlyCannonDeNobilitatibusSapientiiEtPrudentiisRegumManuscriptWalterdeMilemete1326.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/EarlyCannonDeNobilitatibusSapientiiEtPrudentiisRegumManuscriptWalterdeMilemete1326.jpg/440px-EarlyCannonDeNobilitatibusSapientiiEtPrudentiisRegumManuscriptWalterdeMilemete1326.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:EarlyCannonDeNobilitatibusSapientiiEtPrudentiisRegumManuscriptWalterdeMilemete1326.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Earliest depiction of a European cannon, "De Nobilitatibus Sapientii Et Prudentiis Regum", <a href="/wiki/Walter_de_Milemete" title="Walter de Milemete">Walter de Milemete</a>, 1326.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:De_la_pirotechnia_1540_Title_Page_AQ1_(1).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5616" data-file-width="3744" decoding="async" height="270" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/De_la_pirotechnia_1540_Title_Page_AQ1_%281%29.jpg/180px-De_la_pirotechnia_1540_Title_Page_AQ1_%281%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/De_la_pirotechnia_1540_Title_Page_AQ1_%281%29.jpg/270px-De_la_pirotechnia_1540_Title_Page_AQ1_%281%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/De_la_pirotechnia_1540_Title_Page_AQ1_%281%29.jpg/360px-De_la_pirotechnia_1540_Title_Page_AQ1_%281%29.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:De_la_pirotechnia_1540_Title_Page_AQ1_(1).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>De la pirotechnia</i>, 1540</div></div></div>
<p>The earliest Western accounts of gunpowder appears in texts written by English philosopher <a href="/wiki/Roger_Bacon" title="Roger Bacon">Roger Bacon</a> in 1267 called <i><a href="/wiki/Opus_Majus" title="Opus Majus">Opus Majus</a></i> and <i>Opus Tertium</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986-46">[46]</a></sup> The oldest written recipes in continental Europe were recorded under the name Marcus Graecus or Mark the Greek between 1280 and 1300 in the <i><a href="/wiki/Liber_Ignium" title="Liber Ignium">Liber Ignium</a></i>, or <i>Book of Fires</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKelly200423_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKelly200423-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some sources mention possible gunpowder weapons being deployed by the Mongols against European forces at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Mohi" title="Battle of Mohi">Battle of Mohi</a> in 1241.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECowley199386_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECowley199386-50">[50]</a></sup> Professor Kenneth Warren Chase credits the Mongols for introducing into Europe gunpowder and its associated weaponry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChase2003_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChase2003-51">[51]</a></sup> However, there is no clear route of transmission,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAndrade201676_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAndrade201676-52">[52]</a></sup> and while the Mongols are often pointed to as the likeliest vector, Timothy May points out that "there is no concrete evidence that the Mongols used gunpowder weapons on a regular basis outside of China."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Timothy_May_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Timothy_May-53">[53]</a></sup> However, Timothy May also points out "However... the Mongols used the gunpowder weapon in their wars against the Jin, the Song and in their invasions of Japan."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Timothy_May_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-Timothy_May-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>Records show that, in England, gunpowder was being made in 1346 at the <a href="/wiki/Tower_of_London" title="Tower of London">Tower of London</a>; a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Powder_house" title="Powder house">powder house</a> existed at the Tower in 1461; and in 1515 three King's gunpowder makers worked there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECocroft2000_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECocroft2000-54">[54]</a></sup> Gunpowder was also being made or stored at other royal castles, such as <a href="/wiki/Portchester_Castle" title="Portchester Castle">Portchester</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">English Civil War</a> (1642–1645) led to an expansion of the gunpowder industry, with the repeal of the Royal Patent in August 1641.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECocroft2000_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECocroft2000-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>In late 14th century Europe, gunpowder was improved by <i>corning</i>, the practice of drying it into small clumps to improve combustion and consistency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKelly200461_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKelly200461-56">[56]</a></sup> During this time, European manufacturers also began regularly purifying saltpeter, using wood ashes containing <a href="/wiki/Potassium_carbonate" title="Potassium carbonate">potassium carbonate</a> to precipitate calcium from their dung liquor, and using ox blood, <a href="/wiki/Alum" title="Alum">alum</a>, and slices of <a href="/wiki/Turnip" title="Turnip">turnip</a> to clarify the solution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKelly200461_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKelly200461-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the Renaissance, two European schools of <a href="/wiki/Pyrotechnics" title="Pyrotechnics">pyrotechnic</a> thought emerged, one in Italy and the other at Nuremberg, Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Asher_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Asher-57">[57]</a></sup> In Italy, <a href="/wiki/Vannoccio_Biringuccio" title="Vannoccio Biringuccio">Vannoccio Biringuccio</a>, born in 1480, was a member of the guild <i>Fraternita di Santa Barbara</i> but broke with the tradition of secrecy by setting down everything he knew in a book titled <i><a href="/wiki/De_la_pirotechnia" title="De la pirotechnia">De la pirotechnia</a></i>, written in vernacular.  It was published posthumously in 1540, with 9 editions over 138 years, and also reprinted by <a href="/wiki/MIT_Press" title="MIT Press">MIT Press</a> in 1966.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKelly200461_56-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKelly200461-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>By the mid-17th century fireworks were used for entertainment on an unprecedented scale in Europe, being popular even at resorts and public gardens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encarta_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Encarta-58">[58]</a></sup> With the publication of <i>Deutliche Anweisung zur Feuerwerkerey</i> (1748), methods for creating fireworks were sufficiently well-known and well-described that "Firework making has become an exact science."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Philip_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Philip-59">[59]</a></sup> In 1774 <a href="/wiki/Louis_XVI" title="Louis XVI">Louis XVI</a> ascended to the throne of France at age 20.  After he discovered that France was not self-sufficient in gunpowder, a Gunpowder Administration was established; to head it, the lawyer <a href="/wiki/Antoine_Lavoisier" title="Antoine Lavoisier">Antoine Lavoisier</a> was appointed. Although from a bourgeois family, after his degree in law Lavoisier became wealthy from a company set up to collect taxes for the Crown; this allowed him to pursue experimental natural science as a hobby.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>Without access to cheap saltpeter (controlled by the British), for hundreds of years France had relied on saltpetremen with royal warrants, the <i>droit de fouille</i> or "right to dig", to seize nitrous-containing soil and demolish walls of barnyards, without compensation to the owners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKelly2004164_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKelly2004164-61">[61]</a></sup> This caused farmers, the wealthy, or entire villages to bribe the petermen and the associated bureaucracy to leave their buildings alone and the saltpeter uncollected.  Lavoisier instituted a crash program to increase saltpeter production, revised (and later eliminated) the <i>droit de fouille</i>, researched best refining and powder manufacturing methods, instituted management and record-keeping, and established pricing that encouraged private investment in works.  Although saltpeter from new Prussian-style putrefaction works had not been produced yet (the process taking about 18 months), in only a year France had gunpowder to export. A chief beneficiary of this surplus was the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolution</a>.  By careful testing and adjusting the proportions and grinding time, powder from mills such as at <a href="/wiki/Essonne" title="Essonne">Essonne</a> outside Paris became the best in the world by 1788, and inexpensive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKelly2004164_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKelly2004164-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>Two British physicists, <a href="/wiki/Sir_Andrew_Noble,_1st_Baronet" title="Sir Andrew Noble, 1st Baronet">Andrew Noble</a> and <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Abel" title="Frederick Abel">Frederick Abel</a>, worked to improve the properties of gunpowder during the late 19th century. This formed the basis for the Noble-Abel gas equation for <a href="/wiki/Internal_ballistics" title="Internal ballistics">internal ballistics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>The introduction of <a href="/wiki/Smokeless_powder" title="Smokeless powder">smokeless powder</a> in the late 19th century led to a contraction of the gunpowder industry. After the end of <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, the majority of the British gunpowder manufacturers merged into a single company, "Explosives Trades limited"; and a number of sites were closed down, including those in Ireland. This company became Nobel Industries Limited; and in 1926 became a founding member of <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Chemical_Industries" title="Imperial Chemical Industries">Imperial Chemical Industries</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Home_Office" title="Home Office">Home Office</a> removed gunpowder from its list of <i>Permitted Explosives</i>; and shortly afterwards, on 31 December 1931, the former <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Curtis_%26_Harvey&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Curtis &amp; Harvey (page does not exist)">Curtis &amp; Harvey</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Glynneath" title="Glynneath">Glynneath</a> gunpowder factory at <a href="/wiki/Pontneddfechan" title="Pontneddfechan">Pontneddfechan</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a>, closed down, and it was demolished by fire in 1932.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-glynneath_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-glynneath-64">[64]</a></sup> The last remaining gunpowder mill at the <a href="/wiki/Waltham_Abbey_Royal_Gunpowder_Mills" title="Waltham Abbey Royal Gunpowder Mills">Royal Gunpowder Factory, Waltham Abbey</a> was damaged by a German <a href="/wiki/Parachute_mine" title="Parachute mine">parachute mine</a> in 1941 and it never reopened.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECocroft2000_54-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECocroft2000-54">[54]</a></sup> This was followed by the closure of the gunpowder section at the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Ordnance_Factory" title="Royal Ordnance Factory">Royal Ordnance Factory</a>, <a href="/wiki/ROF_Chorley" title="ROF Chorley">ROF Chorley</a>, the section was closed and demolished at the end of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>; and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nobel_Industries_(Scotland)" title="Nobel Industries (Scotland)">ICI Nobel</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Roslin,_Midlothian" title="Roslin, Midlothian">Roslin</a> gunpowder factory, which closed in 1954.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECocroft2000_54-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECocroft2000-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-macdougall_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-macdougall-65">[65]</a></sup> This left ICI Nobel's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ardeer,_Scotland" title="Ardeer, Scotland">Ardeer</a> site in <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, which included a gunpowder factory, as the only factory in Great Britain producing gunpowder. The gunpowder area of the Ardeer site closed in October 1976.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECocroft2000_54-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECocroft2000-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="India">India</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gunpowder&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: India">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rocket_warfare.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="722" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Rocket_warfare.jpg/220px-Rocket_warfare.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Rocket_warfare.jpg/330px-Rocket_warfare.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Rocket_warfare.jpg/440px-Rocket_warfare.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rocket_warfare.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In the year 1780 the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_East_India_Company" title="British East India Company">British</a> began to <a href="/wiki/Annexation" title="Annexation">annex</a> the territories of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Mysore" title="Sultanate of Mysore">Sultanate of Mysore</a>, during the <a href="/wiki/Second_Anglo-Mysore_War" title="Second Anglo-Mysore War">Second Anglo-Mysore War</a>. The British battalion was defeated during the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Guntur&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Battle of Guntur (page does not exist)">Battle of Guntur</a>, by the forces of <a href="/wiki/Hyder_Ali" title="Hyder Ali">Hyder Ali</a>, who effectively utilized <a href="/wiki/Mysorean_rockets" title="Mysorean rockets">Mysorean rockets</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rocket_artillery" title="Rocket artillery">rocket artillery</a> against the closely massed British forces.</div></div></div>
<p>Gunpowder and gunpowder weapons were transmitted to India through the <a href="/wiki/Mongol_invasions_of_India" title="Mongol invasions of India">Mongol invasions of India</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kn_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kn-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kn2_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-kn2-67">[67]</a></sup> The Mongols were defeated by <a href="/wiki/Alauddin_Khalji" title="Alauddin Khalji">Alauddin Khalji</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Delhi_Sultanate" title="Delhi Sultanate">Delhi Sultanate</a>, and some of the Mongol soldiers remained in northern India after their conversion to Islam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kn2_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-kn2-67">[67]</a></sup> It was written in the <i>Tarikh-i Firishta</i> (1606–1607) that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nasiruddin_Mahmud_(grandson_of_Iltutmish)" title="Nasiruddin Mahmud (grandson of Iltutmish)">Nasiruddin Mahmud</a> the ruler of the Delhi Sultanate presented the envoy of the Mongol ruler <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hulegu_Khan" title="Hulegu Khan">Hulegu Khan</a> with a dazzling pyrotechnics display upon his arrival in <a href="/wiki/Delhi" title="Delhi">Delhi</a> in 1258. Nasiruddin Mahmud tried to express his strength as a ruler and tried to ward off any <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mongol" title="Mongol">Mongol</a> attempt similar to the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Baghdad_(1258)" title="Siege of Baghdad (1258)">Siege of Baghdad (1258)</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKhan20049–10_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKhan20049–10-68">[68]</a></sup> Firearms known as <i>top-o-tufak</i> also existed in many Muslim kingdoms in India by as early as 1366.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKhan20049–10_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKhan20049–10-68">[68]</a></sup> From then on the employment of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gunpowder_warfare" title="Gunpowder warfare">gunpowder warfare</a> in India was prevalent, with events such as the "Siege of <a href="/wiki/Belgaum" title="Belgaum">Belgaum</a>" in 1473 by <a href="/wiki/Sultan" title="Sultan">Sultan</a> Muhammad Shah Bahmani.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKhan200410_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKhan200410-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>The shipwrecked Ottoman <a href="/wiki/Admiral" title="Admiral">Admiral</a> <a href="/wiki/Seydi_Ali_Reis" title="Seydi Ali Reis">Seydi Ali Reis</a> is known to have introduced the earliest type of <a href="/wiki/Matchlock" title="Matchlock">matchlock</a> weapons, which the Ottomans used against the <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portuguese</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Diu_(1531)" title="Siege of Diu (1531)">Siege of Diu (1531)</a>. After that, a diverse variety of firearms, large guns in particular, became visible in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tanjore" title="Tanjore">Tanjore</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dacca" title="Dacca">Dacca</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adil_Shahi" title="Adil Shahi">Bijapur</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Murshidabad" title="Murshidabad">Murshidabad</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPartington1999225_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPartington1999225-70">[70]</a></sup> Guns made of bronze were recovered from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_Kozhikode" title="History of Kozhikode">Calicut</a> (1504)- the former capital of the <a href="/wiki/Zamorin" title="Zamorin">Zamorins</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPartington1999226_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPartington1999226-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Meister_der_Sh%C3%A2h-Jah%C3%A2n-N%C3%A2ma-Memoiren_001.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1881" data-file-width="1248" decoding="async" height="271" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Meister_der_Sh%C3%A2h-Jah%C3%A2n-N%C3%A2ma-Memoiren_001.jpg/180px-Meister_der_Sh%C3%A2h-Jah%C3%A2n-N%C3%A2ma-Memoiren_001.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Meister_der_Sh%C3%A2h-Jah%C3%A2n-N%C3%A2ma-Memoiren_001.jpg/270px-Meister_der_Sh%C3%A2h-Jah%C3%A2n-N%C3%A2ma-Memoiren_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Meister_der_Sh%C3%A2h-Jah%C3%A2n-N%C3%A2ma-Memoiren_001.jpg/360px-Meister_der_Sh%C3%A2h-Jah%C3%A2n-N%C3%A2ma-Memoiren_001.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Meister_der_Sh%C3%A2h-Jah%C3%A2n-N%C3%A2ma-Memoiren_001.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mughal_Emperor" title="Mughal Emperor">Mughal Emperor</a> <a href="/wiki/Shah_Jahan" title="Shah Jahan">Shah Jahan</a>, hunting deer using a <a href="/wiki/Matchlock" title="Matchlock">matchlock</a></div></div></div>
<p>The Mughal emperor <a href="/wiki/Akbar" title="Akbar">Akbar</a> mass-produced matchlocks for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mughal_Army" title="Mughal Army">Mughal Army</a>. Akbar is personally known to have shot a leading <a href="/wiki/Rajput" title="Rajput">Rajput</a> commander during the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Chittorgarh_(1567%E2%80%931568)" title="Siege of Chittorgarh (1567–1568)">Siege of Chittorgarh</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughals</a> began to use <a href="/wiki/Bamboo" title="Bamboo">bamboo</a> rockets (mainly for signalling) and employ <a href="/wiki/Sapper" title="Sapper">sappers</a>: special units that undermined heavy stone fortifications to plant gunpowder charges.
</p><p>The Mughal Emperor <a href="/wiki/Shah_Jahan" title="Shah Jahan">Shah Jahan</a> is known to have introduced much more advanced matchlocks, their designs were a combination of Ottoman and Mughal designs. Shah Jahan also countered the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British</a> and other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Europeans" title="Europeans">Europeans</a> in his province of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gujar%C4%81t" title="Gujarāt">Gujarāt</a>, which supplied Europe saltpeter for use in gunpowder warfare during the 17th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IndiaBritannica_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-IndiaBritannica-73">[73]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bengal" title="Bengal">Bengal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malwa" title="Malwa">Mālwa</a> participated in saltpeter production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IndiaBritannica_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-IndiaBritannica-73">[73]</a></sup> The Dutch, French, Portuguese, and English used <a href="/wiki/Chhapra" title="Chhapra">Chhapra</a> as a center of saltpeter refining.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IndiaBritannica_73-2"><a href="#cite_note-IndiaBritannica-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ever since the founding of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Mysore" title="Sultanate of Mysore">Sultanate of Mysore</a> by <a href="/wiki/Hyder_Ali" title="Hyder Ali">Hyder Ali</a>, <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">French</a> military officers were employed to train the Mysore Army. Hyder Ali and his son <a href="/wiki/Tipu_Sultan" title="Tipu Sultan">Tipu Sultan</a> were the first to introduce modern cannons and <a href="/wiki/Musket" title="Musket">muskets</a>, their army was also the first in India to have official uniforms. During the <a href="/wiki/Second_Anglo-Mysore_War" title="Second Anglo-Mysore War">Second Anglo-Mysore War</a> Hyder Ali and his son <a href="/wiki/Tipu_Sultan" title="Tipu Sultan">Tipu Sultan</a> unleashed the Mysorean rockets at their British opponents effectively defeating them on various occasions. The Mysorean rockets inspired the development of the <a href="/wiki/Congreve_rocket" title="Congreve rocket">Congreve rocket</a>, which the British widely utilized during the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a> and the <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-r&amp;ms_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-r&amp;ms-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Southeast_Asia">Southeast Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gunpowder&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Southeast Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Madrid_canons_indiens.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2500" data-file-width="3800" decoding="async" height="184" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Madrid_canons_indiens.png/280px-Madrid_canons_indiens.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Madrid_canons_indiens.png/420px-Madrid_canons_indiens.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Madrid_canons_indiens.png/560px-Madrid_canons_indiens.png 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Madrid_canons_indiens.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A double barrelled cetbang on a carriage, with swivel yoke, ca. 1522. The mouth of the cannon is in the shape of <a href="/wiki/N%C4%81ga#Indonesia" title="Nāga">Javanese Nāga</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Cannons were introduced to Majapahit when <a href="/wiki/Kublai_Khan" title="Kublai Khan">Kublai Khan's</a> Chinese army under the leadership of Ike Mese <a href="/wiki/Mongol_invasion_of_Java" title="Mongol invasion of Java">sought to invade Java</a> in 1293. <a href="/wiki/History_of_Yuan" title="History of Yuan">History of Yuan</a> mentioned that the Mongol used cannons (Chinese: <a href="/wiki/Hu_dun_pao" title="Hu dun pao">炮—<i>Pào</i></a>) against Daha forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schlegel_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schlegel-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 1–2">: 1–2 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:11_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-:11-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 220">: 220 </span></sup> Cannons were used by the <a href="/wiki/Ayutthaya_Kingdom" title="Ayutthaya Kingdom">Ayutthaya Kingdom</a> in 1352 during its invasion of the <a href="/wiki/Khmer_Empire" title="Khmer Empire">Khmer Empire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPurton2010201_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPurton2010201-78">[78]</a></sup> Within a decade large quantities of gunpowder could be found in the <a href="/wiki/Khmer_Empire" title="Khmer Empire">Khmer Empire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPurton2010201_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPurton2010201-78">[78]</a></sup> By the end of the century firearms were also used by the <a href="/wiki/Tr%E1%BA%A7n_dynasty" title="Trần dynasty">Trần dynasty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETran200675_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETran200675-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>Even though the knowledge of making gunpowder-based weapon has been known after the failed Mongol invasion of Java, and the predecessor of firearms, the <a href="/wiki/Hand_cannon" title="Hand cannon">pole gun</a> (<a href="/wiki/Bedil_tombak" title="Bedil tombak">bedil tombak</a>), was recorded as being used by Java in 1413,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:72_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-:72-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 245">: 245 </span></sup> the knowledge of making "true" firearms came much later, after the middle of the 15th century. It was brought by the <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islamic</a> nations of West Asia, most probably the <a href="/wiki/Arabs" title="Arabs">Arabs</a>. The precise year of introduction is unknown, but it may be safely concluded to be no earlier than 1460.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:22_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-:22-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 23">: 23 </span></sup> Before the arrival of the Portuguese in Southeast Asia, the natives already possessed primitive firearms, the <a href="/wiki/Java_arquebus" title="Java arquebus">Java arquebus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-:02-83">[83]</a></sup> Portuguese influence to local weaponry, particularly after the <a href="/wiki/Capture_of_Malacca_(1511)" title="Capture of Malacca (1511)">capture of Malacca (1511)</a>, resulted in a new type of hybrid tradition matchlock firearm, the <a href="/wiki/Istinggar" title="Istinggar">istinggar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>Portuguese and Spanish invaders were unpleasantly surprised and even outgunned on occasion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> Circa 1540, the Javanese, always alert for new weapons found the newly arrived Portuguese weaponry superior to that of the locally made variants. <a href="/wiki/Majapahit" title="Majapahit">Majapahit</a>-era cetbang cannons were further improved and used in the <a href="/wiki/Demak_Sultanate" title="Demak Sultanate">Demak Sultanate</a> period during the Demak invasion of <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Malacca" title="Portuguese Malacca">Portuguese Malacca</a>. During this period, the <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> for manufacturing Javanese cannons was imported from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khorasan_Province" title="Khorasan Province">Khorasan</a> in northern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">Persia</a>. The material was known by Javanese as <i>wesi kurasani</i> (Khorasan iron).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> When the <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Empire" title="Portuguese Empire">Portuguese</a> came to the archipelago, they referred to it as <i>berço</i>, which was also used to refer to any breech-loading swivel gun, while the <a href="/wiki/Spaniards" title="Spaniards">Spaniards</a> call it <i>verso</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 151">: 151 </span></sup> By the early 16th century, the Javanese already locally-producing large guns, some of them still survived until the present day and dubbed as "sacred cannon" or "holy cannon". These cannons varied between 180- to 260-pounders, weighing anywhere between 3–8 tons, length of them between 3–6 m.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>Saltpeter harvesting was recorded by Dutch and German travelers as being common in even the smallest villages and was collected from the decomposition process of large dung hills specifically piled for the purpose. The Dutch punishment for possession of non-permitted gunpowder appears to have been amputation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:13_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-:13-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 180–181">: 180–181 </span></sup> Ownership and manufacture of gunpowder was later prohibited by the colonial <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Dutch</a> occupiers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dipanegara,_P._B._R_19812_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dipanegara,_P._B._R_19812-90">[90]</a></sup> According to colonel McKenzie quoted in Sir <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thomas_Stamford_Raffles" title="Thomas Stamford Raffles">Thomas Stamford Raffles</a>', <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_History_of_Java_(1817_book)" title="The History of Java (1817 book)">The History of Java</a></i> (1817), the purest sulfur was supplied from <a href="/wiki/Ijen" title="Ijen">a crater from a mountain</a> near the straits of <a href="/wiki/Bali" title="Bali">Bali</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:13_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-:13-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 180–181">: 180–181 </span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Historiography">Historiography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gunpowder&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Historiography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Historiography_of_gunpowder_and_gun_transmission" title="Historiography of gunpowder and gun transmission">Historiography of gunpowder and gun transmission</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ph%C3%A1o_th%E1%BB%A7.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="671" decoding="async" height="258" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Ph%C3%A1o_th%E1%BB%A7.jpg/180px-Ph%C3%A1o_th%E1%BB%A7.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Ph%C3%A1o_th%E1%BB%A7.jpg/270px-Ph%C3%A1o_th%E1%BB%A7.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Ph%C3%A1o_th%E1%BB%A7.jpg/360px-Ph%C3%A1o_th%E1%BB%A7.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ph%C3%A1o_th%E1%BB%A7.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gunner of <a href="/wiki/Nguy%E1%BB%85n_dynasty" title="Nguyễn dynasty">Nguyễn dynasty</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a></div></div></div>
<p>On the origins of gunpowder technology, historian <a href="/wiki/Tonio_Andrade" title="Tonio Andrade">Tonio Andrade</a> remarked, "Scholars today overwhelmingly concur that the gun was invented in China."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAndrade201675_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAndrade201675-91">[91]</a></sup> Gunpowder and the gun are widely believed by historians to have originated from China due to the large body of evidence that documents the evolution of gunpowder from a medicine to an incendiary and explosive, and the evolution of the gun from the <a href="/wiki/Fire_lance" title="Fire lance">fire lance</a> to a metal gun, whereas similar records do not exist elsewhere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAndrade201675–76_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAndrade201675–76-92">[92]</a></sup> As Andrade explains, the large amount of variation in gunpowder recipes in China relative to Europe is "evidence of experimentation in China, where gunpowder was at first used as an incendiary and only later became an explosive and a propellant... in contrast, formulas in Europe diverged only very slightly from the ideal proportions for use as an explosive and a propellant, suggesting that gunpowder was introduced as a mature technology."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAndrade201676_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAndrade201676-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, the history of gunpowder is not without controversy. A major problem confronting the study of early gunpowder history is ready access to sources close to the events described. Often the first records potentially describing use of gunpowder in warfare were written several centuries after the fact, and may well have been colored by the contemporary experiences of the chronicler.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEÁgoston200815_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEÁgoston200815-93">[93]</a></sup> Translation difficulties have led to errors or loose interpretations bordering on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artistic_licence" title="Artistic licence">artistic licence</a>. Ambiguous language can make it difficult to distinguish gunpowder weapons from similar technologies that do not rely on gunpowder. A commonly cited example is a report of the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Mohi" title="Battle of Mohi">Battle of Mohi</a> in Eastern Europe that mentions a "long lance" sending forth "evil-smelling vapors and smoke", which has been variously interpreted by different historians as the "first-gas attack upon European soil" using gunpowder, "the first use of cannon in Europe", or merely a "toxic gas" with no evidence of gunpowder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPartington1999198Saunders1971198_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPartington1999198Saunders1971198-94">[94]</a></sup> It is difficult to accurately translate original Chinese alchemical texts, which tend to explain phenomena through metaphor, into modern scientific language with rigidly defined terminology in English. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKelly200422_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKelly200422-24">[24]</a></sup> Early texts potentially mentioning gunpowder are sometimes marked by a linguistic process where <a href="/wiki/Semantic_change" title="Semantic change">semantic change</a> occurred.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPartington1999xvi–xvii_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPartington1999xvi–xvii-95">[95]</a></sup> For instance, the Arabic word <i>naft</i> transitioned from denoting <a href="/wiki/Naphtha" title="Naphtha">naphtha</a> to denoting gunpowder, and the Chinese word <i>pào</i> changed in meaning from trebuchet to a cannon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPurton2010108–09_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPurton2010108–09-96">[96]</a></sup> This has led to arguments on the exact origins of gunpowder based on etymological foundations. Science and technology historian Bert S. Hall makes the observation that, "It goes without saying, however, that historians bent on special pleading, or simply with axes of their own to grind, can find rich material in these terminological thickets."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPartington1999xvi–xvii_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPartington1999xvi–xvii-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another major area of contention in modern studies of the history of gunpowder is regarding the transmission of gunpowder. While the literary and archaeological evidence supports a Chinese origin for gunpowder and guns, the manner in which gunpowder technology was transferred from China to the West is still under debate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAndrade201675_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAndrade201675-91">[91]</a></sup> It is unknown why the rapid spread of gunpowder technology across Eurasia took place over several decades whereas other technologies such as paper, the compass, and printing did not reach Europe until centuries after they were invented in China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAndrade201676_52-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAndrade201676-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Components">Components</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gunpowder&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Components">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Gunpowder is a granular mixture of:
</p>
<ul><li>a <a href="/wiki/Nitrate" title="Nitrate">nitrate</a>, typically potassium nitrate (KNO<sub>3</sub>), which supplies oxygen for the reaction;</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Charcoal" title="Charcoal">charcoal</a>, which provides carbon and other fuel for the reaction, simplified as carbon (C);</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> (S), which, while also serving as a fuel, lowers the temperature required to ignite the mixture, thereby increasing the rate of <a href="/wiki/Combustion" title="Combustion">combustion</a>.</li></ul>
<p>Potassium nitrate is the most important ingredient in terms of both bulk and function because the combustion process releases oxygen from the potassium nitrate, promoting the rapid burning of the other ingredients.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBuchanan20064_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBuchanan20064-97">[97]</a></sup> To reduce the likelihood of accidental ignition by <a href="/wiki/Static_electricity" title="Static electricity">static electricity</a>, the granules of modern gunpowder are typically coated with <a href="/wiki/Graphite" title="Graphite">graphite</a>, which prevents the build-up of electrostatic charge.
</p><p>Charcoal does not consist of pure carbon; rather, it consists of partially <a href="/wiki/Pyrolysis" title="Pyrolysis">pyrolyzed</a> <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">cellulose</a>, in which the wood is not completely decomposed. <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">Carbon</a> differs from ordinary <a href="/wiki/Charcoal" title="Charcoal">charcoal</a>. Whereas charcoal's autoignition temperature is relatively low, carbon's is much greater. Thus, a gunpowder composition containing pure carbon would burn similarly to a match head, at best.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>The current standard composition for the gunpowder manufactured by pyrotechnicians was adopted as long ago as 1780. Proportions by weight are 75% potassium nitrate (known as saltpeter or saltpetre), 15% softwood charcoal, and 10% sulfur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-earl-2_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-earl-2-99">[99]</a></sup> These ratios have varied over the centuries and by country, and can be altered somewhat depending on the purpose of the powder. For instance, power grades of black powder, unsuitable for use in firearms but adequate for blasting rock in quarrying operations, are called blasting powder rather than gunpowder with standard proportions of 70% nitrate, 14% charcoal, and 16% sulfur; blasting powder may be made with the cheaper <a href="/wiki/Sodium_nitrate" title="Sodium nitrate">sodium nitrate</a> substituted for potassium nitrate and proportions may be as low as 40% nitrate, 30% charcoal, and 30% sulfur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> In 1857, Lammot du Pont solved the main problem of using cheaper sodium nitrate formulations when he patented DuPont "B" blasting powder. After manufacturing grains from press-cake in the usual way, his process tumbled the powder with graphite dust for 12 hours. This formed a graphite coating on each grain that reduced its ability to absorb moisture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKelly2004218_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKelly2004218-101">[101]</a></sup>
</p><p>Neither the use of graphite nor sodium nitrate was new. Glossing gunpowder corns with graphite was already an accepted technique in 1839,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> and sodium nitrate-based blasting powder had been made in Peru for many years using the sodium nitrate mined at <a href="/wiki/Tarapac%C3%A1_Province_(Peru)" title="Tarapacá Province (Peru)">Tarapacá</a> (now in Chile).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> Also, in 1846, two plants were built in south-west England to make blasting powder using this sodium nitrate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> The idea may well have been brought from Peru by Cornish miners returning home after completing their contracts. Another suggestion is that it was <a href="/wiki/William_Lobb" title="William Lobb">William Lobb</a>, the plant collector, who recognised the possibilities of sodium nitrate during his travels in South America. Lammot du Pont would have known about the use of graphite and probably also knew about the plants in south-west England. In his patent he was careful to state that his claim was for the combination of graphite with sodium nitrate-based powder, rather than for either of the two individual technologies.
</p><p>French war powder in 1879 used the ratio 75% saltpeter, 12.5% charcoal, 12.5% sulfur. English war powder in 1879 used the ratio 75% saltpeter, 15% charcoal, 10% sulfur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> The British Congreve rockets used 62.4% saltpeter, 23.2% charcoal and 14.4% sulfur, but the British Mark VII gunpowder was changed to 65% saltpeter, 20% charcoal and 15% sulfur.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The explanation for the wide variety in formulation relates to usage. Powder used for rocketry can use a slower burn rate since it accelerates the projectile for a much longer time—whereas powders for weapons such as flintlocks, cap-locks, or matchlocks need a higher burn rate to accelerate the projectile in a much shorter distance. Cannons usually used lower burn-rate powders, because most would burst with higher burn-rate powders.
