</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_compositions">Other compositions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gunpowder&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Other compositions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Besides black powder, there are other historically important types of gunpowder. "Brown gunpowder" is cited as composed of 79% nitre, 3% sulfur, and 18% charcoal per 100 of dry powder, with about 2% moisture. <a href="/wiki/Brown_powder" title="Brown powder">Prismatic Brown Powder</a> is a large-grained product the <a href="/wiki/Rottweil" title="Rottweil">Rottweil</a> Company introduced in 1884 in Germany, which was adopted by the British Royal Navy shortly thereafter. The French navy adopted a fine, 3.1 millimeter, not prismatic grained product called <i>Slow Burning Cocoa</i> (SBC) or "cocoa powder". These brown powders reduced burning rate even further by using as little as 2 percent sulfur and using charcoal made from <a href="/wiki/Rye" title="Rye">rye</a> straw that had not been completely charred, hence the brown color.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKelly2004224_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKelly2004224-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p><p>Lesmok powder was a product developed by DuPont in 1911,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> one of several semi-smokeless products in the industry containing a mixture of black and nitrocellulose powder. It was sold to <a href="/wiki/Winchester_Repeating_Arms_Company" title="Winchester Repeating Arms Company">Winchester</a> and others primarily for .22 and .32 small calibers. Its advantage was that it was believed at the time to be less corrosive than smokeless powders then in use.  It was not understood in the U.S. until the 1920s that the actual source of corrosion was the potassium chloride residue from potassium chlorate sensitized primers.  The bulkier black powder fouling better disperses primer residue.  Failure to mitigate primer corrosion by dispersion caused the false impression that nitrocellulose-based powder caused corrosion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> Lesmok had some of the bulk of black powder for dispersing primer residue, but somewhat less total bulk than straight black powder, thus requiring less frequent bore cleaning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wakeman_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wakeman-109">[109]</a></sup> It was last sold by Winchester in 1947.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sulfur-free_powders">Sulfur-free powders</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gunpowder&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Sulfur-free powders">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Burst_muzzle_loader_barrel.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1095" data-file-width="3504" decoding="async" height="88" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Burst_muzzle_loader_barrel.jpg/280px-Burst_muzzle_loader_barrel.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Burst_muzzle_loader_barrel.jpg/420px-Burst_muzzle_loader_barrel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Burst_muzzle_loader_barrel.jpg/560px-Burst_muzzle_loader_barrel.jpg 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Burst_muzzle_loader_barrel.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Burst barrel of a muzzle loader pistol replica, which was loaded with nitrocellulose powder instead of black powder and could not withstand the higher pressures of the modern propellant</div></div></div>
<p>The development of smokeless powders, such as <a href="/wiki/Cordite" title="Cordite">cordite</a>, in the late 19th century created the need for a spark-sensitive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blasting_cap" title="Blasting cap">priming charge</a>, such as gunpowder. However, the sulfur content of traditional gunpowders caused <a href="/wiki/Corrosion" title="Corrosion">corrosion</a> problems with Cordite Mk I and this led to the introduction of a range of sulfur-free gunpowders, of varying grain sizes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECocroft2000_54-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECocroft2000-54">[54]</a></sup> They typically contain 70.5 parts of saltpeter and 29.5 parts of charcoal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECocroft2000_54-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECocroft2000-54">[54]</a></sup> Like black powder, they were produced in different grain sizes. In the United Kingdom, the finest grain was known as <i>sulfur-free mealed powder</i> (<i>SMP</i>). Coarser grains were numbered as sulfur-free gunpowder (SFG n): 'SFG 12', 'SFG 20', 'SFG 40' and 'SFG 90', for example; where the number represents the smallest BSS sieve mesh size, which retained no grains.
</p><p>Sulfur's main role in gunpowder is to decrease the ignition temperature. A sample reaction for sulfur-free gunpowder would be:
</p>
<dl><dd>6 KNO<sub>3</sub> + C<sub>7</sub>H<sub>4</sub>O → 3 K<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> + 4 CO<sub>2</sub> + 2 H<sub>2</sub>O + 3 N<sub>2</sub></dd></dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Smokeless_powders">Smokeless powders</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gunpowder&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Smokeless powders">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The term <i>black powder</i> was coined in the late 19th century, primarily in the United States, to distinguish prior gunpowder formulations from the new smokeless powders and semi-smokeless powders. Semi-smokeless powders featured bulk volume properties that approximated black powder, but had significantly reduced amounts of smoke and combustion products. Smokeless powder has different burning properties (pressure vs. time) and can generate higher pressures and work per gram. This can rupture older weapons designed for black powder. Smokeless powders ranged in color from brownish tan to yellow to white. Most of the bulk semi-smokeless powders ceased to be manufactured in the 1920s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wakeman_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wakeman-109">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Granularity">Granularity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gunpowder&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Granularity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Serpentine">Serpentine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gunpowder&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Serpentine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The original dry-compounded powder used in 15th-century Europe was known as "Serpentine", either a reference to Satan<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENolan2006365_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENolan2006365-27">[27]</a></sup> or to a common artillery piece that used it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKelly200458_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKelly200458-112">[112]</a></sup> The ingredients were ground
together with a mortar and pestle, perhaps for 24 hours,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKelly200458_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKelly200458-112">[112]</a></sup> resulting in a fine flour.  Vibration during transportation could cause the components to separate again, requiring remixing in the field. Also if the quality of the saltpeter was low (for instance if it was contaminated with highly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hygroscopic" title="Hygroscopic">hygroscopic</a> <a href="/wiki/Calcium_nitrate" title="Calcium nitrate">calcium nitrate</a>), or if the powder was simply old (due to the mildly hygroscopic nature of potassium nitrate), in humid weather it would need to be re-dried. The dust from "repairing" powder in the field was a major hazard.
</p><p>Loading cannons or <a href="/wiki/Bombard_(weapon)" title="Bombard (weapon)">bombards</a> before the powder-making advances of the Renaissance was a skilled art. Fine powder loaded haphazardly or too tightly would burn incompletely or too slowly. Typically, the breech-loading powder chamber in the rear of the piece was filled only about half full, the serpentine powder neither too compressed nor too loose, a wooden bung pounded in to seal the chamber from the barrel when assembled, and the projectile placed on.  A carefully determined empty space was necessary for the charge to burn effectively. When the cannon was fired through the touchhole, turbulence from the initial surface combustion caused the rest of the powder to be rapidly exposed to the flame.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKelly200458_112-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKelly200458-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>The advent of much more powerful and easy to use <i>corned</i> powder changed this procedure, but serpentine was used with older guns into the 17th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guilmartin2003_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-Guilmartin2003-113">[113]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Corning">Corning</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gunpowder&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Corning">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>For propellants to oxidize and burn rapidly and effectively, the combustible ingredients must be reduced to the smallest possible particle sizes, and be as thoroughly mixed as possible. Once mixed, however, for better results in a gun, makers discovered that the final product should be in the form of individual dense grains that spread the fire quickly from grain to grain, much as <a href="/wiki/Straw" title="Straw">straw</a> or <a href="/wiki/Twig" title="Twig">twigs</a> catch fire more quickly than a pile of <a href="/wiki/Sawdust" title="Sawdust">sawdust</a>.
</p><p>In late 14th century Europe and China,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAndrade2016110_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAndrade2016110-114">[114]</a></sup> gunpowder was improved by wet grinding; liquid, such as distilled spirits<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKelly200461_56-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKelly200461-56">[56]</a></sup> was added during the grinding-together of the ingredients and the moist paste dried afterwards. The principle of wet mixing to prevent the separation of dry ingredients, invented for gunpowder, is used today in the pharmaceutical industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> It was discovered that if the paste was rolled into balls before drying the resulting gunpowder absorbed less water from the air during storage and traveled better. The balls were then crushed in a mortar by the gunner immediately before use, with the old problem of uneven particle size and packing causing unpredictable results. If the right size particles were chosen, however, the result was a great improvement in power.  Forming the damp paste into <i>corn</i>-sized clumps by hand or with the use of a sieve instead of larger balls produced a product after drying that loaded much better, as each tiny piece provided its own surrounding air space that allowed much more rapid combustion than a fine powder.  This "corned" gunpowder was from 30% to 300% more powerful. An example is cited where 15 kilograms (34 lb) of serpentine was needed to shoot a 21-kilogram (47 lb) ball, but only 8.2 kilograms (18 lb) of corned powder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKelly200461_56-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKelly200461-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>Because the dry powdered ingredients must be mixed and bonded together for extrusion and cut into grains to maintain the blend, size reduction and mixing is done while the ingredients are damp, usually with water. After 1800, instead of forming grains by hand or with sieves, the damp <i>mill-cake</i> was pressed in molds to increase its density and extract the liquid, forming <i>press-cake</i>. The pressing took varying amounts of time, depending on conditions such as atmospheric humidity.  The hard, dense product was broken again into tiny pieces, which were separated with sieves to produce a uniform product for each purpose: coarse powders for cannons, finer grained powders for muskets, and the finest for small hand guns and priming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guilmartin2003_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-Guilmartin2003-113">[113]</a></sup> Inappropriately fine-grained powder often caused cannons to burst before the projectile could move down the barrel, due to the high initial spike in pressure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> <i>Mammoth</i> powder with large grains, made for <a href="/wiki/Rodman_gun" title="Rodman gun">Rodman's 15-inch cannon</a>, reduced the pressure to only 20 percent as high as ordinary cannon powder would have produced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKelly2004195_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKelly2004195-117">[117]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the mid-19th century, measurements were made determining that the burning rate within a grain of black powder (or a tightly packed mass) is about 6 cm/s (0.20 feet/s), while the rate of ignition propagation from grain to grain is around 9 m/s (30 feet/s), over two orders of magnitude faster.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guilmartin2003_113-2"><a href="#cite_note-Guilmartin2003-113">[113]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_types">Modern types</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gunpowder&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Modern types">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hexagonal_Gunpowder.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="700" data-file-width="700" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Hexagonal_Gunpowder.png/170px-Hexagonal_Gunpowder.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Hexagonal_Gunpowder.png/255px-Hexagonal_Gunpowder.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Hexagonal_Gunpowder.png/340px-Hexagonal_Gunpowder.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hexagonal_Gunpowder.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Hexagonal gunpowder for large artillery</div></div></div>
<p>Modern corning first compresses the fine black powder meal into blocks with a fixed density (1.7 g/cm<sup>3</sup>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> In the United States, gunpowder grains were designated F (for fine) or C (for coarse). Grain diameter decreased with a larger number of Fs and increased with a larger number of Cs, ranging from about <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1020198016">.mw-parser-output .frac{white-space:nowrap}.mw-parser-output .frac .num,.mw-parser-output .frac .den{font-size:80%;line-height:0;vertical-align:super}.mw-parser-output .frac .den{vertical-align:sub}.mw-parser-output .sr-only{border:0;clip:rect(0,0,0,0);height:1px;margin:-1px;overflow:hidden;padding:0;position:absolute;width:1px}</style>2 mm (<span class="frac" role="math"><span class="num">1</span>⁄<span class="den">16</span></span> in) for 7F to <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1020198016" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/>15 mm (<span class="frac" role="math"><span class="num">9</span>⁄<span class="den">16</span></span> in) for 7C. Even larger grains were produced for artillery bore diameters greater than about 17 cm (6.7 in). The standard DuPont <i>Mammoth</i> powder developed by Thomas Rodman and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lammot_du_Pont" title="Lammot du Pont">Lammot du Pont</a> for use during the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a> had grains averaging 15 mm (0.6 in) in diameter with edges rounded in a glazing barrel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKelly2004195_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKelly2004195-117">[117]</a></sup> Other versions had grains the size of golf and tennis balls for use in 20-inch (51 cm) <a href="/wiki/Rodman_gun" title="Rodman gun">Rodman guns</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brown_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-brown-119">[119]</a></sup> In 1875 DuPont introduced <i>Hexagonal</i> powder for large artillery, which was pressed using shaped plates with a small center core—about <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1020198016" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/>38 mm (<span class="frac" role="math">1<span class="sr-only">+</span><span class="num">1</span>⁄<span class="den">2</span></span> in) diameter, like a wagon wheel nut, the center hole widened as the grain burned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKelly2004224_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKelly2004224-106">[106]</a></sup> By 1882 German makers also produced hexagonal grained powders of a similar size for artillery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKelly2004224_106-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKelly2004224-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p><p>By the late 19th century manufacturing focused on standard grades of black powder from Fg used in large bore rifles and shotguns, through FFg (medium and small-bore arms such as muskets and fusils), FFFg (small-bore rifles and pistols), and FFFFg (extreme small bore, short pistols and most commonly for priming <a href="/wiki/Flintlock" title="Flintlock">flintlocks</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-James2011_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-James2011-120">[120]</a></sup> A coarser grade for use in military artillery <a href="/wiki/Blank_(cartridge)" title="Blank (cartridge)">blanks</a> was designated A-1. These grades were sorted on a system of screens with oversize retained on a mesh of 6 wires per inch, A-1 retained on 10 wires per inch, Fg retained on 14, FFg on 24, FFFg on 46, and FFFFg on 60. Fines designated FFFFFg were usually reprocessed to minimize explosive dust hazards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>, the main service gunpowders were classified RFG (rifle grained fine) with diameter of one or two millimeters and RLG (rifle grained large) for grain diameters between two and six millimeters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brown_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-brown-119">[119]</a></sup> Gunpowder grains can alternatively be categorized by mesh size: the BSS <a href="/wiki/Mesh_(scale)" title="Mesh (scale)">sieve mesh size</a>, being the smallest mesh size, which retains no grains. Recognized grain sizes are Gunpowder G 7, G 20, G 40, and G 90.
</p><p>Owing to the large market of antique and replica black-powder firearms in the US, modern  <a href="/wiki/Black_powder_substitute" title="Black powder substitute">black powder substitutes</a> like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pyrodex" title="Pyrodex">Pyrodex</a>, Triple Seven and Black Mag3<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wakeman_109-2"><a href="#cite_note-Wakeman-109">[109]</a></sup> pellets have been developed since the 1970s. These products, which should not be confused with smokeless powders, aim to produce less fouling (solid residue), while maintaining the traditional volumetric measurement system for charges. Claims of less corrosiveness of these products have been controversial however. New cleaning products for black-powder guns have also been developed for this market.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-James2011_120-1"><a href="#cite_note-James2011-120">[120]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemistry">Chemistry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gunpowder&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Chemistry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>A simple, commonly cited, <a href="/wiki/Chemical_equation" title="Chemical equation">chemical equation</a> for the combustion of gunpowder is:
</p>
<dl><dd>2 <a href="/wiki/Potassium_nitrate" title="Potassium nitrate">KNO<sub>3</sub></a> + <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">S</a> + 3 <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">C</a> → <a href="/wiki/Potassium_sulfide" title="Potassium sulfide">K<sub>2</sub>S</a> + <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">N<sub>2</sub></a> + 3 <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">CO<sub>2</sub></a>.</dd></dl>
<p>A balanced, but still simplified, equation is:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>10 <a href="/wiki/Potassium_nitrate" title="Potassium nitrate">KNO<sub>3</sub></a> + 3 <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">S</a> + 8 <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">C</a> → 2 <a href="/wiki/Potassium_carbonate" title="Potassium carbonate">K<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub></a> + 3 <a href="/wiki/Potassium_sulfate" title="Potassium sulfate">K<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub></a> + 6 <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">CO<sub>2</sub></a> + 5 <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">N<sub>2</sub></a>.</dd></dl>
<p>The exact percentages of ingredients varied greatly through the medieval period as the recipes were developed by trial and error, and needed to be updated for changing military technology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERitchieRiegnerSealsRogers2021_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERitchieRiegnerSealsRogers2021-123">[123]</a></sup>
</p><p>Gunpowder does not burn as a single reaction, so the byproducts are not easily predicted. One study<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> showed that it produced (in order of descending quantities) 55.91% solid products: <a href="/wiki/Potassium_carbonate" title="Potassium carbonate">potassium carbonate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Potassium_sulfate" title="Potassium sulfate">potassium sulfate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Potassium_sulfide" title="Potassium sulfide">potassium sulfide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Potassium_nitrate" title="Potassium nitrate">potassium nitrate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Potassium_thiocyanate" title="Potassium thiocyanate">potassium thiocyanate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ammonium_carbonate" title="Ammonium carbonate">ammonium carbonate</a> and 42.98% gaseous products: <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>, 1.11% water.
</p><p>Gunpowder made with less-expensive and more plentiful sodium nitrate instead of potassium nitrate (in appropriate proportions) works just as well. However, it is more <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hygroscopic" title="Hygroscopic">hygroscopic</a> than powders made from potassium nitrate. <a href="/wiki/Muzzleloader" title="Muzzleloader">Muzzleloaders</a> have been known to fire after hanging on a wall for decades in a loaded state, provided they remained dry. By contrast, gunpowder made with sodium nitrate must be kept sealed to remain stable.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="The material near this tag possibly contains original research. (October 2021)">original research?</span></a></i>]</sup>
Gunpowder releases 3 <a href="/wiki/Joule#Megajoule" title="Joule">megajoules</a> per <a href="/wiki/Kilogram" title="Kilogram">kilogram</a> and contains its own oxidant.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> This is less than <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trinitrotoluene" title="Trinitrotoluene">TNT</a> (4.7 megajoules per kilogram), or <a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">gasoline</a> (47.2 megajoules per kilogram in combustion, but gasoline requires an oxidant; for instance, an optimized gasoline and O<sub>2</sub> mixture releases 10.4 megajoules per kilogram, taking into account the mass of the oxygen).
</p><p>Gunpowder also has a low <a href="/wiki/Energy_density" title="Energy density">energy density</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Cleanup" title="Wikipedia:Cleanup"><span title="Wikipedia:Cleanup">how much?</span></a></i>]</sup> compared to modern "smokeless" powders, and thus to achieve high energy loadings, large amounts are needed with heavy projectiles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gunpowder&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Powder_mill" title="Powder mill">Powder mill</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hagley_Mill_Equipment.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="533" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Hagley_Mill_Equipment.jpg/220px-Hagley_Mill_Equipment.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Hagley_Mill_Equipment.jpg/330px-Hagley_Mill_Equipment.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Hagley_Mill_Equipment.jpg/440px-Hagley_Mill_Equipment.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hagley_Mill_Equipment.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Edge-runner mill in a restored mill, at <a href="/wiki/Hagley_Museum_and_Library" title="Hagley Museum and Library">The Hagley Museum</a></div></div></div>

<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Martello_Tower_barrels.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1248" data-file-width="1664" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Martello_Tower_barrels.jpg/220px-Martello_Tower_barrels.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Martello_Tower_barrels.jpg/330px-Martello_Tower_barrels.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Martello_Tower_barrels.jpg/440px-Martello_Tower_barrels.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Martello_Tower_barrels.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gunpowder storing barrels at the <a href="/wiki/Martello_tower" title="Martello tower">Martello tower</a> in <a href="/wiki/Point_Pleasant_Park" title="Point Pleasant Park">Point Pleasant Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Halifax,_Nova_Scotia" title="Halifax, Nova Scotia">Halifax, Nova Scotia</a>, Canada</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Barout_khaneh_near_Tehran_by_Eug%C3%A8ne_Flandin.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1071" data-file-width="1797" decoding="async" height="131" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Barout_khaneh_near_Tehran_by_Eug%C3%A8ne_Flandin.jpg/220px-Barout_khaneh_near_Tehran_by_Eug%C3%A8ne_Flandin.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Barout_khaneh_near_Tehran_by_Eug%C3%A8ne_Flandin.jpg/330px-Barout_khaneh_near_Tehran_by_Eug%C3%A8ne_Flandin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Barout_khaneh_near_Tehran_by_Eug%C3%A8ne_Flandin.jpg/440px-Barout_khaneh_near_Tehran_by_Eug%C3%A8ne_Flandin.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Barout_khaneh_near_Tehran_by_Eug%C3%A8ne_Flandin.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1840 drawing of a <a href="/wiki/Gunpowder_magazine" title="Gunpowder magazine">gunpowder magazine</a> near <a href="/wiki/Tehran" title="Tehran">Tehran</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">Persia</a>. Gunpowder was extensively used in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Naderian_Wars" title="Naderian Wars">Naderian Wars</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>For the most powerful black powder, <a href="/wiki/Meal_powder" title="Meal powder">meal powder</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Wood" title="Wood">wood</a> charcoal, is used. The best wood for the purpose is Pacific <a href="/wiki/Willow" title="Willow">willow</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> but others such as <a href="/wiki/Alder" title="Alder">alder</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buckthorn" title="Buckthorn">buckthorn</a> can be used. In Great Britain between the 15th and 19th centuries charcoal from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alder_buckthorn" title="Alder buckthorn">alder buckthorn</a> was greatly prized for gunpowder manufacture; <a href="/wiki/Populus_sect._Aigeiros" title="Populus sect. Aigeiros">cottonwood</a> was used by the American <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Confederate_States" title="Confederate States">Confederate States</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKelly2004200_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKelly2004200-127">[127]</a></sup> The ingredients are reduced in particle size and mixed as intimately as possible. Originally, this was with a mortar-and-pestle or a similarly operating stamping-mill, using copper, bronze or other non-sparking materials, until supplanted by the rotating <a href="/wiki/Ball_mill" title="Ball mill">ball mill</a> principle with non-sparking <a href="/wiki/Bronze" title="Bronze">bronze</a> or <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a>. Historically, a marble or <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a> edge runner mill, running on a limestone bed, was used in Great Britain; however, by the mid 19th century this had changed to either an iron-shod stone wheel or a <a href="/wiki/Cast_iron" title="Cast iron">cast iron</a> wheel running on an iron bed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-earl-2_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-earl-2-99">[99]</a></sup> The mix was dampened with <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">alcohol</a> or water during grinding to prevent accidental ignition. This also helps the extremely soluble saltpeter to mix into the microscopic pores of the very high surface-area charcoal.
</p><p>Around the late 14th century, European powdermakers first began adding liquid during grinding to improve mixing, reduce dust, and with it the risk of explosion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKelly200460–63_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKelly200460–63-128">[128]</a></sup> The powder-makers would then shape the resulting paste of dampened gunpowder, known as mill cake, into corns, or grains, to dry. Not only did corned powder keep better because of its reduced surface area, gunners also found that it was more powerful and easier to load into guns. Before long, powder-makers standardized the process by forcing mill cake through sieves instead of corning powder by hand.
</p><p>The improvement was based on reducing the surface area of a higher density composition. At the beginning of the 19th century, makers increased density further by static pressing. They shoveled damp mill cake into a two-foot square box, placed this beneath a screw press and reduced it to half its volume. "Press cake" had the hardness of <a href="/wiki/Slate" title="Slate">slate</a>. They broke the dried slabs with hammers or rollers, and sorted the granules with sieves into different grades.  In the United States, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eleuthere_Irenee_du_Pont" title="Eleuthere Irenee du Pont">Eleuthere Irenee du Pont</a>, who had learned the trade from Lavoisier, tumbled the dried grains in rotating barrels to round the edges and increase durability during shipping and handling. (Sharp grains rounded off in transport, producing fine "meal dust" that changed the burning properties.)
</p><p>Another advance was the manufacture of kiln charcoal by distilling wood in heated iron retorts instead of burning it in earthen pits. Controlling the temperature influenced the power and consistency of the finished gunpowder.  In 1863, in response to high prices for Indian saltpeter, <a href="/wiki/DuPont" title="DuPont">DuPont</a> chemists developed a process using <a href="/wiki/Potash" title="Potash">potash</a> or mined <a href="/wiki/Potassium_chloride" title="Potassium chloride">potassium chloride</a> to convert plentiful <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chilean</a> sodium nitrate to potassium nitrate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKelly2004199_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKelly2004199-129">[129]</a></sup>
</p><p>The following year (1864) the Gatebeck Low Gunpowder Works in Cumbria (Great Britain) started a plant to manufacture potassium nitrate by essentially the same chemical process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> This is nowadays called the 'Wakefield Process', after the owners of the company. It would have used potassium chloride from the Staßfurt mines, near Magdeburg, Germany, which had recently become available in industrial quantities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the 18th century, gunpowder factories became increasingly dependent on mechanical energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> Despite mechanization, production difficulties related to humidity control, especially during the pressing, were still present in the late 19th century. A paper from 1885 laments that "Gunpowder is such a nervous and sensitive spirit, that in almost every process of manufacture it changes under our hands as the weather changes." Pressing times to the desired density could vary by a factor of three depending on the atmospheric humidity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legal_status">Legal status</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gunpowder&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Legal status">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> Model Regulations on the Transportation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dangerous_Goods" title="Dangerous Goods">Dangerous Goods</a> and national transportation authorities, such as <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Transportation" title="United States Department of Transportation">United States Department of Transportation</a>, have classified gunpowder (black powder) as a <i>Group A: Primary explosive substance</i> for shipment because it ignites so easily. Complete manufactured devices containing black powder are usually classified as <i>Group D: Secondary detonating substance, or black powder, or article containing secondary detonating substance</i>, such as firework, class D <a href="/wiki/Model_rocket" title="Model rocket">model rocket</a> engine, etc., for shipment because they are harder to ignite than loose powder. As explosives, they all fall into the category of Class 1 .
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_uses">Other uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gunpowder&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Other uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Besides its use as a propellant in firearms and artillery, black powder's other main use has been as a blasting powder in quarrying, mining, and road construction (including railroad construction). During the 19th century, outside of war emergencies such as the Crimean War or the American Civil War, more black powder was used in these industrial uses than in firearms and artillery. <a href="/wiki/Dynamite" title="Dynamite">Dynamite</a> gradually replaced it for those uses. Today, industrial explosives for such uses are still a huge market, but most of the market is in newer explosives rather than black powder.
</p><p>Beginning in the 1930s, gunpowder or smokeless powder was used in <a href="/wiki/Rivet_gun" title="Rivet gun">rivet guns</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stunning" title="Stunning">stun guns</a> for animals, cable splicers and other industrial construction tools.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> The "stud gun", a <a href="/wiki/Powder-actuated_tool" title="Powder-actuated tool">powder-actuated tool</a>, drove nails or screws into solid concrete, a function not possible with hydraulic tools, and today is still an important part of various industries, but the cartridges usually use smokeless powders. Industrial <a href="/wiki/Shotgun" title="Shotgun">shotguns</a> have been used to eliminate persistent material rings in operating rotary kilns (such as those for cement, lime, phosphate, etc.) and clinker in operating furnaces, and commercial tools make the method more reliable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup>
</p><p>Gunpowder has occasionally been employed for other purposes besides weapons, mining, fireworks and construction:
</p>
<ul><li>After the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Aspern-Essling" title="Battle of Aspern-Essling">Battle of Aspern-Essling</a> (1809), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dominique-Jean_Larrey" title="Dominique-Jean Larrey">Dominique-Jean Larrey</a>, the surgeon of the Napoleonic Army, lacking salt, seasoned a <a href="/wiki/Horse_meat" title="Horse meat">horse meat</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bouillon_(broth)" title="Bouillon (broth)">bouillon</a> for the wounded under his care with gunpowder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Parker_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-Parker-136">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Musee_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-Musee-137">[137]</a></sup> It was also used for sterilization in ships when there was no alcohol.</li>
<li>British sailors used gunpowder to create <a href="/wiki/Tattoo" title="Tattoo">tattoos</a> when ink wasn't available, by pricking the skin and rubbing the powder into the wound in a method known as traumatic tattooing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Christiaan_Huygens" title="Christiaan Huygens">Christiaan Huygens</a> experimented with gunpowder in 1673 in an early attempt to build an <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engine" title="Internal combustion engine">internal combustion engine</a>, but he did not succeed. Modern attempts to recreate his invention were similarly unsuccessful.</li>
<li>Near London in 1853, Captain Shrapnel demonstrated a <a href="/wiki/Mineral_processing" title="Mineral processing">mineral processing</a> use of black powder in a method for crushing gold-bearing ores by firing them from a cannon into an iron chamber,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and "much satisfaction was expressed by all present". He hoped it would be useful on the <a href="/wiki/Gold_rush" title="Gold rush">goldfields</a> of <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a> and <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>. Nothing came of the invention, as continuously-operating crushing machines that achieved more reliable <a href="/wiki/Comminution" title="Comminution">comminution</a> were already coming into use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup></li>
<li>Starting in 1967, Los Angeles-based artist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ed_Ruscha" title="Ed Ruscha">Ed Ruscha</a> began using gunpowder as an artistic medium for a series of works on paper.</li></ul>
